<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer8628714596559514">With <em>Empire Falls</em> Richard Russo cements his reputation as one of America’s most compelling and compassionate storytellers. <br /><br />Miles Roby has been slinging burgers at the Empire Grill for 20 years, a job that cost him his college education and much of his self-respect. What keeps him there? It could be his bright, sensitive daughter Tick, who needs all his help surviving the l</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8628714596559514">With <em>Empire Falls</em> Richard Russo cements his reputation as one of America’s most compelling and compassionate storytellers. <br /><br />Miles Roby has been slinging burgers at the Empire Grill for 20 years, a job that cost him his college education and much of his self-respect. What keeps him there? It could be his bright, sensitive daughter Tick, who needs all his help surviving the local high school. Or maybe it’s Janine, Miles’s soon-to-be ex-wife, who’s taken up with a noxiously vain health-club proprietor. Or perhaps it’s the imperious Francine Whiting, who owns everything in town--and seems to believe that “everything” includes Miles himself. In <em>Empire Falls</em> Richard Russo delves deep into the blue-collar heart of America in a work that overflows with hilarity, heartache, and grace.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8628714596559514">...more</a>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_162813208">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/162813208" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5300620-steve-sckenda" class="left imgcol" title="Steve Sckenda"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1409027039p2/5300620.jpg" alt="Steve Sckenda" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/162813208?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 04, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5300620-steve-sckenda" class="user" title="Steve Sckenda">Steve Sckenda</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">The Defeated </span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Steve by: </span>
      Pulitzer Prize for Fiction
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5300620-steve-sckenda?shelf=20th-century" class="actionLinkLite">20th-century</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5300620-steve-sckenda?shelf=new-england" class="actionLinkLite">new-england</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5300620-steve-sckenda?shelf=fathers-and-daughters" class="actionLinkLite">fathers-and-daughters</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5300620-steve-sckenda?shelf=mothers-and-sons" class="actionLinkLite">mothers-and-sons</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5300620-steve-sckenda?shelf=pulitzer-prize" class="actionLinkLite">pulitzer-prize</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5300620-steve-sckenda?shelf=miniseries" class="actionLinkLite">miniseries</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5300620-steve-sckenda?shelf=television" class="actionLinkLite">television</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer162813208">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13123370521135257959">“<em>The simple truth is not all of us become the men we once hoped we might be.</em>” --Master and Commander, Patrick O'Brian.<br /><br /><strong>Miles Roby did not grow up to become the man he once hoped he might be. How do you measure success? </strong> Accomplishments? Wealth? Distance Travelled? Kindness? Character? Once the brightest boy in his class, Miles flips burgers at his diner, The Empire Grill, which he leases from Mrs. Whiting, the town’s domineering heiress who runs the town as her fiefdom. <br /><br />Though Miles considers him</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13123370521135257959">“<em>The simple truth is not all of us become the men we once hoped we might be.</em>” --Master and Commander, Patrick O'Brian.<br /><br /><strong>Miles Roby did not grow up to become the man he once hoped he might be. How do you measure success? </strong> Accomplishments? Wealth? Distance Travelled? Kindness? Character? Once the brightest boy in his class, Miles flips burgers at his diner, The Empire Grill, which he leases from Mrs. Whiting, the town’s domineering heiress who runs the town as her fiefdom. <br /><br />Though Miles considers himself a failure, he is a decent man and a doting father to his teenage daughter, Tick. Miles quit college to care for his dying mother, and now he spends his free time painting the rectory at St. Catherine’s; looks after his alcoholic father and disabled brother; and is a loyal friend to an emotionally disturbed and disabled woman. Miles loses money at the grill while employing others with luck worse than his own. <br /><br />Miles (a man with an <em>“overdeveloped sense of responsibility”</em>) contrasts with his rakish father, Max (<em>“no slave to conscience”</em>). Max’s advice: <em>“don’t get greedy; steal small” </em> because you can get away with the small grift. Miles dreams of escaping Empire Falls, but the sacrificial virtue he inherited from his deceased mother does not allow him to follow his irresponsible father’s road to “freedom.” <br /><br />The other main character is Empire Falls, Maine, a rusty, industrial town. Like Miles, Empire Falls is not the home that its residents once hoped it might be. The global economy killed the logging and textile mills. The residents, forced out of manufacturing into serfdom and low-paying service jobs, invent fantasies of rich investors reopening the shuttered mills and saving the town. <br /><br />Richard Russo explores the psyche of the defeated: <blockquote> <em> After all, what was the whole world but a place for people to yearn for their heart’s impossible desires, for those desires to become entrenched in defiance of logic, plausibility, and even passage of time, as eternal as polished marble. (295) </em>
</blockquote> Russo won the Pulitzer Prize for fiction in 2002 for this tragic-comic social novel that abounds with compassion and gentle humor. He serves many sub-plots involving other parents and children at the tasty grill of "Empire Falls," and Russo inserts strategic flashbacks (told in italics) about Miles’ youth and the secrets of the Whiting family. <br /><br />This novel forced me to ask: Is happiness too much of a burden (<em>“a carcinogenic”</em>) for some people? Can those who believe in the Great Chain of Being ever aspire to a place outside the chain of command? How do we escape serfdom? How do we measure the success of a life? I ask these questions because I did not become the man I once hoped I might be. To accept this with good cheer, I like to read about alleged middle-aged “failures” like Miles Roby to discover the reliable metrics of success.<br /><br />Herodotus warned: “call no man happy until his death." My corollary is probably true: proclaim no person a failure until death. We may yet become the humans we once hoped to be. Until then, it is necessary that someone should be on the side of the defeated. <br /><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380348776i/702924.jpg" /><br />Ed Harris as Miles Roby and Paul Newman as Max in the HBO miniseries "Empire Falls"<br /><br /><br /><br /><br />******* <br />Here is a link to my review of “The Shipping News,” another Pulitzer Prize novel in which Quoyle, another a middle-aged man, struggles against his sense of failure. <br /><br /><a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/162813653" target="_blank">http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/...</a><br /><br />December 4, 2012</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13123370521135257959">...more</a>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown">
    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_991946842">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/991946842" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/405390-kemper" class="left imgcol" title="Kemper"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1451574879p2/405390.jpg" alt="Kemper" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/991946842?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 04, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/405390-kemper" class="user" title="Kemper">Kemper</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=plain-old-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">plain-old-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=modern-lit" class="actionLinkLite">modern-lit</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=2014" class="actionLinkLite">2014</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=small-town" class="actionLinkLite">small-town</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer991946842">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6093819118573214040">Empire Falls, Maine is a town that’s best days are long behind it. The mill and factory that used to be the main employers have been closed for years, and the only person around with two dimes to rub together is the very rich Francine Whiting who essentially owns and controls everything worth having in the area. Miles Robey was on the verge of earning his college degree and escaping Empire Falls forever when he returned home to care for his dying mother and ended up working for Mrs. Whiting as t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6093819118573214040">Empire Falls, Maine is a town that’s best days are long behind it. The mill and factory that used to be the main employers have been closed for years, and the only person around with two dimes to rub together is the very rich Francine Whiting who essentially owns and controls everything worth having in the area. Miles Robey was on the verge of earning his college degree and escaping Empire Falls forever when he returned home to care for his dying mother and ended up working for Mrs. Whiting as the manager of the Empire Grill.<br /><br />Two decades later and Miles is a middle aged punching bag still slinging burgers who probably bursts into tears every time he hears Pearl Jam’s <em>Elderly Woman Behind the Counter in a Small Town</em>. His selfish wife Janine is divorcing Miles to marry health club owner Walt Comeau who likes to stop by the Empire Grill every afternoon to rub it in. Miles’ father Max is the town neer-do-well who is impervious to guilt and is constantly trying to get money out of him. Town cop Jimmy Minty starts approaching Miles under the guise of friendship but has some long simmering grudge against him. Worst of all is that Miles has to report the slender profits to Mrs. Whiting even as she refuses to pay for any improvements to the increasingly shabby diner. The one bright spot in life for Miles is his teen-aged daughter Tick who he loves dearly and has a close relationship with.<br /><br />This is the first book I’ve read by Russo (Although I’ve seen the movie version of <em>Nobody’s Fool</em>.), and I absolutely loved it. At least until the ending, but we’ll get to that shortly. The depiction of a small blue collar town slowly going under was done incredibly well from the opening that describes how Empire Falls has been ruled by the Whitings for generations and how heir C.B. went from his dream of wanting to be a poet and artist in Mexico to running the family business and marrying Francine.<br /><br />The characters are another big selling point because there’s a lot more than I described in the brief summary above, and all of them seem rich and fully developed. It’s to Russo’s credit that he was able to make a cast that includes some absolutely infuriating and unpleasant people and make you at least understand all of them. There were times where I wished that Miles would push his father out of a moving car or toss a pan full of hot grease into the face of Walt, but there was also a certain obnoxious charm to them most of the time.<br /><br />Miles is the heart of the book, and I was a little worried that I wasn’t going to like him much in the early going. I’m generally not a fan of passive characters that are so wrapped up in regrets and unearned guilt that they’re essentially just pawns for anyone looking to use them, and Miles fits this description to a T. Being raised Catholic by his selfless mother has convinced him that wanting anything is practically a sin, and he’s almost pathologically incapable of standing up for himself. However, Miles’ brother is constantly calling him out for taking the path of least resistance and urging him to at least try to change his circumstances. That awareness of his nature and the flashes of backbone that Miles shows at times make him sympathetic despite being pretty much a doormat.<br /><br />As far as the ending <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[ I loved that Mrs. Whiting had essentially turned Miles into an indentured servant because of the affair his mother had with her husband. Miles seemed exceptionally slow on the uptake there, but the hardest place to see a trap is from inside it.<br /><br />However, I was less pleased with other elements. John Voss shooting the people in the high school was the kind of dramatic moment that it would take for Miles to make a big gesture like leaving Empire Falls to protect Tick. So while I wasn’t sold on the idea of this character driven story about small town secrets and regrets turning into a ripped-from-the-headlines tragedy, it made sense in that respect. But I didn’t like how everything got kind of deus ex machina after that. Mrs. Whiting drowns, Jimmy Minty is disgraced, Zach gets shipped off to Seattle, Janine leaves Walt all while David and Bea get the new restaurant going. That all just seemed a little too convenient for Miles. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Despite those complaints this was still an exceptionally well written book with that did a great job establishing and exploring all the tangled relationships in one dying town, and it has enough humor to keep everything from getting overly grim and depressing for the most part. It’s easy to see why it won a Pulitzer. <br /><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6093819118573214040">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/245244699?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 22, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4426615-jason" class="user" title="Jason">Jason</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4426615-jason?shelf=for-kindle" class="actionLinkLite">for-kindle</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4426615-jason?shelf=2011" class="actionLinkLite">2011</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4426615-jason?shelf=reviewed" class="actionLinkLite">reviewed</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer245244699">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11653671392983521315">I’ve really fallen in love with the characters in this one.<br /><br />To me, the most difficult thing to do in literature is develop a character. Character-driven novels are a gamble because if they are not handled correctly, they can crash and burn before they’ve taken off. Plot-driven novels are a safer bet, but then you’d miss out on an opportunity to really provoke your reader. I liked <em>Empire Falls</em> primarily because of how real Miles and Max and Mrs. Whiting, etc. felt to me.<br /><br />This book encompasses what</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11653671392983521315">I’ve really fallen in love with the characters in this one.<br /><br />To me, the most difficult thing to do in literature is develop a character. Character-driven novels are a gamble because if they are not handled correctly, they can crash and burn before they’ve taken off. Plot-driven novels are a safer bet, but then you’d miss out on an opportunity to really provoke your reader. I liked <em>Empire Falls</em> primarily because of how real Miles and Max and Mrs. Whiting, etc. felt to me.<br /><br />This book encompasses what it must have been like (although I’m only imagining here) to live in a small New England town suffering through a period of economic decay. It is depressing, and yet it isn’t—<em>because</em> of the characters. Somehow Miles inspires hope in the reader, and Max’s frustrating personality actually serves to make a point (in addition to providing comic relief), which is that you don’t actually <em>have</em> to take life so seriously. I mean, you can if you want. But you don’t have to. And even Mrs. Whiting speaks volumes. She may be vicious and manipulative on the surface, but the more the reader discovers about her, the more he is able to empathize with her. She has struggled more than any other character in the novel and yet she presents herself stoically. How very “New England” of her. And so do we find out for sure whether there’s a heart of gold underneath all that armor? Nope! Because really, not much happens in this story plot-wise. But it almost doesn’t matter because it is the great set of characters in this book that, for me, made it most enjoyable.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11653671392983521315">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown">
    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_10325029">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/10325029" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/613434-linda" class="left imgcol" title="Linda"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1194764957p2/613434.jpg" alt="Linda" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/10325029?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 16, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/613434-linda" class="user" title="Linda">Linda</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everybody, but especially those with more than a passing acquaintance with small-town life</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/613434-linda?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer10325029">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15670987847689499423"><em>This was a book my brother really enjoyed and recommended to me as recently as this summer. So it went on my list. :o)</em><br /><br /><em>My brother passed away on October 9, 2007. Today (well, since it's after midnight, technically, yesterday) is his birthday, so it seems fitting that I've finally gotten around to posting this review today. </em><br /><br />When I finish a book, I find I kind of have to let things simmer in my brain a bit before I can really parse out all my reactions to it. I’m not sure why, but this one took me</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15670987847689499423"><em>This was a book my brother really enjoyed and recommended to me as recently as this summer. So it went on my list. :o)</em><br /><br /><em>My brother passed away on October 9, 2007. Today (well, since it's after midnight, technically, yesterday) is his birthday, so it seems fitting that I've finally gotten around to posting this review today. </em><br /><br />When I finish a book, I find I kind of have to let things simmer in my brain a bit before I can really parse out all my reactions to it. I’m not sure why, but this one took me a little longer than usual. I think that it may be that the book didn’t really have one big point to make – rather, it was more an interweaving of lots of little points, that all weave together in a portrait of everyday life in a particular time and place.<br /><br />I liked the book tremendously. I enjoyed the pace and the mood, and felt the author has a very natural sense of tone that communicates volumes about his characters and their situations in a mostly unobtrusive and seamless way. I also appreciated that he was able to write many of his characters in such a way that the reader is able to like them and sometimes empathize with them, even when they are not <em>being</em> particularly likeable. I found that I had developed an affection for characters even when they were occasionally – or more than occasionally – annoying. Kind of like with real people I know. :) This is a feature I really liked in the novel.<br /><br />My first and most frequent response as I was reading the book was to hear in my head Thoreau’s line that “The mass of men lead lives of quiet desperation.” Empire Falls is clearly a whole town living lives of quiet desperation. Though I think Thoreau meant it in terms of people being trapped by the daily grind, or leading an unexamined, insufficiently spiritually-nurturing kind of life. Whereas I mean it more in the sense of someone become ossified in ordinariness, having given up on their dreams, or not daring to dream at all. Miles Roby, the central character of Empire Falls, gave up on his dreams as a young man, and has become rooted in Empire Falls. Although he doesn’t walk around despairing of his life or choices, and does keep alive some hopes (however unrealistic) for the future, he has become settled into his small town life and narrow-bordered existence. Several other characters are undone by disappointed dreams – Grace Roby, who once gets tantalizingly close to the realization of a dream, only to have the rest of her life tainted by its failure to realize; Janine Roby, who leaves her husband in pursuit of a dream that ends up disappointing her; Tick Roby, who is in serious danger of giving up on her dreams before she has really even had the chance to take them out for a spin.<br /><br />Inertia plays a key role in this sense of quiet desperation in the novel – inertia at times braided with wishful thinking. Over and over again, we see characters stuck in a particular pattern of behavior, with apparently no prospect for breaking out of their patterns. The characters that are the most tragic or pathetic (I’m not sure which, or even if it’s possible to distinguish between the two) are those who are or become trapped by their own wishful thinking. Miles, again, is the chief example of this, but you also see it in Cindy Whiting, Janine, Charlie Whiting, and even Max Roby. And, inertia in this story is true to its nature – it takes an out-of-the-ordinary event to knock loose inertia and set things back in motion.<br /><br />Which is all beginning to sound like a fairly gloomy book, as I look over what I’ve written. But it’s not doom and gloom. It is, rather, a very human story about human relationships. And as much as you see in the book about human foibles, you also see moments of ordinary human grace. On the one hand, the book is full of all the secrets – petty and not so petty – that populate daily life, whether we keep them out of personal shame, or because of our uncertainty about whether telling or keeping a secret is more hurtful to others. On the other hand, the book is also full of quiet acts of self-sacrifice that some of these secrets entail, whether in the keeping or the telling (for example, David Roby keeping a secret from his brother in order to protect him.)<br /><br />My one dissatisfaction with the book (not bad enough to call it a complaint) had to do with the final act. Although there needed to be a turning point event to knock Miles Roby out of his inertia, I think the author went a bit over the top. I didn’t think it was terrible, but I do think that was the one weak spot of the story.<br /><br />Two random, disconnected thoughts that I wanted to mention but I couldn’t find anywhere to fit these in nicely into my review: <br /><br />Disconnected Thought 1: Francine Whiting reminds me of no one more than the Mary Carson character in The Thorn Birds, especially as played by Barbara Stanwyck.<br /><br />Disconnected Thought 2: There is a brilliant moment in Miles' final scene with Cindy Whiting, in which Cindy calls Miles out for pitying her, and for automatically assuming that she had never had any other romantic possibilities. Up until that moment, Miles has only ever seen Cindy as a cut-out, taking for granted that she had no existence beyond him, and suddenly, in an instant, she is revealed as a three-dimensional human being. That moment is so surprising for the reader it almost catches your breath, and the reader is left feeling supremely uncomfortable, as uncomfortable as Miles is at that moment, because the reader is exposed right alongside Miles – as Cindy's accusation reveals exactly what we have been thinking all along.<br /><br />Finally one quote, which actually stands in for a much longer recurring motif:<br /><br />“The passenger seat and floor of the Jetta were now paint-flecked, thanks to Max’s refusal to change into clean clothes when they quit for the day. He made no distinction between work clothes and other clothes, and since he had started helping Miles at St. Cat’s, the old man’s shirts and pants and shoes were all paint-smudged. When people pointed this out, he offered his customary “So what?” Few men, Miles reflected, lived so comfortably within the confines of a two-word personal philosophy.”<br /><br />-- pg. 204-205<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15670987847689499423">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_14407286">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14407286" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/764994-rick" class="left imgcol" title="Rick"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1289746453p2/764994.jpg" alt="Rick" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/14407286?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 23, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/764994-rick" class="user" title="Rick">Rick</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/764994-rick?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer14407286'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer14407286">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3165842300658852332">Big novel about a little place in Maine. The town is in decline and the novel presents numerous characters and plots and overtells most of them, rather than let everyone speak for himself or circumstances to reveal meaning, the author piles on the evidence and looks in on character’s thinking without revealing their complexity. If scenes don’t repeat themselves, what they represent about the characters or fate of the town and its people do. Too much of the storytelling is pedestrian, though Russ</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3165842300658852332">Big novel about a little place in Maine. The town is in decline and the novel presents numerous characters and plots and overtells most of them, rather than let everyone speak for himself or circumstances to reveal meaning, the author piles on the evidence and looks in on character’s thinking without revealing their complexity. If scenes don’t repeat themselves, what they represent about the characters or fate of the town and its people do. Too much of the storytelling is pedestrian, though Russo can handle both description and characterization ably when he wants to, he often settles for a speedwritten, colloquial prose that doesn’t come to life.<br /><br />And there is a meanness embedded in his descriptions of some characters (the Whitings, the Mintys, Janine Roby and her new husband) that borders on hypocrisy when balanced with the forbearance he presents some characters, whose behaviors, or at least motives seem not too far different from the less favored. Janine, for example, seems as abandoned and mis-served by marriage as the mother of Miles Roby, her husband, had been served by her marriage. One is tragic and the other pathetic. Despite going out of its way to make sure we share seemingly everyone’s opinion, including her mom and her daughter, that Janine is a fool, there is never any effort to elicit sympathy for a woman who became involved with a man who never loved her, worked cheek-by-cheek with the woman he always loved, and who has never managed to step up to the plate in any significant way.<br /><br />The book ends with a long anticipated and digressively presented Columbine shooting and, in its wake, at least the possibility that Janine and Miles may reconcile and the town’s fortunes are rebounding sufficiently to allow life to go on. None of it seems organic, just one writer’s idea of a complicated story played out in a small town with some emblematic meaning for our post-industrial world.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3165842300658852332">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_48372401">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/48372401" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/36042-jennifer" class="left imgcol" title="Jennifer"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1260504081p2/36042.jpg" alt="Jennifer" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/48372401?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 12, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/36042-jennifer" class="user" title="Jennifer">Jennifer</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer48372401">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14139626176702771436">This was the BEST book I've read in quite a while. I had actually seen the HBO miniseries based on this a few years back and enjoyed it but didn't think about reading the book until someone told me it's everything Twilight isn't: well-written, complicated, fully-developed characters, good story, strong dialogue, etc, etc.<br />Sold! <br />That's not to say there's any reason to compare it to Twilight. It's not about vampires or in the same genre or league. <br />The Maine town of Empire Falls is probably like man</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14139626176702771436">This was the BEST book I've read in quite a while. I had actually seen the HBO miniseries based on this a few years back and enjoyed it but didn't think about reading the book until someone told me it's everything Twilight isn't: well-written, complicated, fully-developed characters, good story, strong dialogue, etc, etc.<br />Sold! <br />That's not to say there's any reason to compare it to Twilight. It's not about vampires or in the same genre or league. <br />The Maine town of Empire Falls is probably like many Rust Belt cities where residents who stayed behind struggle to get by and wonder what they missed by not leaving. Miles, the main character, almost made it out when he went away to college, but he came home to care for his dying mother. Two decades later he's still managing the local diner, where much of the novel is based. His wife has left him for the owner of the local gym, the woman he's loved since high school thinks he's too nice a guy to ever date. It'd be easy to feel sorry for him, but he's too smart and pulled together to take pity on and you know they're the ones losing out.<br />The novel moves at a small town pace. But honestly, the plot, while it holds together and left me wondering what was coming, isn't that important. There's a lot going on, but I didn't feel overwhelmed trying to keep up with everything. It's like Russo has dropped the reader into this world to grab a seat at the diner and watch small town life unfold. But it's not really the cliche small town life we all know, it's more modern and tarnished. Russo writes so beautifully and with empathy for his characters, who are allowed to have conflicting emotions and behavior, sometimes all at the same time. By the end I was sad to leave because I knew these people and they were so enjoyable to follow. I really hated Max for most of the book, but then it's not that his character changes, but you see him in a new light and by then end, I liked him, though he still drove me crazy. <br />I was a bit envious of Russo who seems to be able to pin people and their feelings down so well. Sure, he's creating these characters so he should know their dearest secrets and every emotion, but they're so realistically written that it feels like he's reading the mind of an actual person.<br />I could go on, but really, just read it. I think I'm in love with this book. <br /><br />Here's a line I love from it that is kind of the point: Lives are rivers. We imagine we can direct their paths, though in the end there's but one destination, and we end up being true to ourselves only because we have no choice.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14139626176702771436">...more</a>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer19168602">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18216596955606532465">I bought this book having only read on the back of it's cover and seeing that it had won the Pulitzer. I half-way expected to find it sligthly boring for that same reason, simpy because I tend to like books with a fair amount of action. And "Empire Falls" has very little action.<br /><br />But man, this book is so well written, I had to stop and curse out loud several times, being a writer myself. Small, everyday situations become intensely interesting, as the web of relationships becomes apparent. It felt</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18216596955606532465">I bought this book having only read on the back of it's cover and seeing that it had won the Pulitzer. I half-way expected to find it sligthly boring for that same reason, simpy because I tend to like books with a fair amount of action. And "Empire Falls" has very little action.<br /><br />But man, this book is so well written, I had to stop and curse out loud several times, being a writer myself. Small, everyday situations become intensely interesting, as the web of relationships becomes apparent. It felt almost like reading a soap opera, but with great actors and a huge budget. I simply had to know, what happened next to the people in this little town.<br /><br />And when the book ended, I still wanted to know more. That more than anything, marks a great novel.<br /><br />The only reason, I don't give it a full five stars, is because of some of the flashback chapters. They serve a purpose, but also took the pace down, sometimes to the point of boredom. When I did put this novel down, it was often during one of these chapters.<br /><br />But all in all, Empire Falls is a great American novel in its original sense. I would recommend it to anyone and everyone.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_75016432">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/75016432" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4695-daniel" class="left imgcol" title="Daniel"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1264806364p2/4695.jpg" alt="Daniel" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/75016432?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 03, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4695-daniel" class="user" title="Daniel">Daniel</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Daniel by: </span>
      Jennifer netherby
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4695-daniel?shelf=2009" class="actionLinkLite">2009</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer75016432">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12445043657834003846">This is one of those rare occasions when I wish GoodReads were to offer half-star ratings, because then I could give "Empire Falls" four and a half stars. It was almost a five-star novel in my estimation, but I had a few quibbles with it. It's nevertheless an excellent book, and one I don't feel much need to review at length; my friend Jennifer, who both recommended the book to me and lent me her copy, has already written a brilliant assessment. Her review is at <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/48372401" target="_blank">http://www.goodreads.com/review/s</a></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12445043657834003846">This is one of those rare occasions when I wish GoodReads were to offer half-star ratings, because then I could give "Empire Falls" four and a half stars. It was almost a five-star novel in my estimation, but I had a few quibbles with it. It's nevertheless an excellent book, and one I don't feel much need to review at length; my friend Jennifer, who both recommended the book to me and lent me her copy, has already written a brilliant assessment. Her review is at <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/48372401" target="_blank">http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/...</a><br /><br />So what are my quibbles? They're not many, really, but I felt that, at times, Russo laid too much out for his readers. There is, for example, a scene in which David Roby details for his brother Miles the personality traits each sibling got from their father and which he received from their mother. It felt like something Russo should have allowed us, his readers, to figure out for ourselves. I wanted Russo to have more trust in his audience. Also, the book seemed to wrap up a bit too neatly; the lives Russo depicted were certainly messy, and they seemed to demand a less tidy conclusion.<br /><br />Still, the book, overall, is a staggering achievement. Russo has an amazing eye for the human condition -- how the way our parents raise us often determines our directions us for the rest of our lives, even if we think we're smart enough to see those risks and do our damnedest to choose our own paths anyhow. (The novel feels, at times, like a book-length meditation on the poem "This Be The Verse," by Philip Larkin: "They fuck you up, your mum and dad. / The may not mean to, but they do. They fill you with the faults they had / And add some extra, just for you.") <br /><br />On a personal note, it was interesting to read Richard Russo's book at the same time I read George Eliot's "Middlemarch." Both, by depicting the lives of tightly knit communities -- the births, deaths love affairs, marriages, educations, careers and dreams both failed and accomplished of the members of those communities -- get at the bigger truths of all our lives. Both Eliot and Russo have a gift for the sardonic, and make us develop an affection for characters who, objectively speaking, we should not like. There's no way we should want to spend time with Russo's Max Roby, yet Russo is such a talented writer that we not only suffer him but develop as much begrudging affection for him as his sons Miles and David have.<br /><br />I've already written more about "Empire Falls" than I intended to. Jennifer nailed it, and you should read her review instead. Then read the book.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12445043657834003846">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8134715285472783204"><strong> 2.5 Stars </strong><br />Empire Falls by Richard Russo is a story about relationships and life in a small town called Empire Falls in Maine.<br /><br />The stroy is extremely well written and the characters are very well developed. I felt at times I was looking in the windows of the characters homes and watching them live life on a daily basis. The relationship between the characters was so well portrayed and a wonderful sense of time and place comes across in the novel. Having said that I still just found the Novel <strong>
  <em>OK </em>
</strong>. I think as the story unfolded and the details of small town life played out it seemed to drag on forever and I became bored and found myself page counting about 3/4 way through the book. What started out as an interesting and entertaining read became tedious and I looked forward to finishing the story. <br /><br />To sum up I found the writing and the character development excellent and I enjoyed how he portrayed relationships in this small town. I just needed a little more drama to to keep me interested.<br /><br /></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_937862313">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/937862313" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/16587936-jr-bacdayan" class="left imgcol" title="Jr Bacdayan"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1358590931p2/16587936.jpg" alt="Jr Bacdayan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/937862313?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 22, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/16587936-jr-bacdayan" class="user" title="Jr Bacdayan">Jr Bacdayan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer937862313">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3269479855549661839">How do you cope with life when all your dreams stayed as they are, just dreams? How do you tell yourself “I have a happy life” when you know that what you yearned will never be? How do you start every morning with a smile? How do you keep afloat?<br /><br />Empire Falls is the story of a small town who dreamed less than they should, and the few people who did and were the worse for it. It’s the story of unrealized dreams, an unrealized love, and an undone marriage. It’s the story of those who stayed, lookin</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3269479855549661839">How do you cope with life when all your dreams stayed as they are, just dreams? How do you tell yourself “I have a happy life” when you know that what you yearned will never be? How do you start every morning with a smile? How do you keep afloat?<br /><br />Empire Falls is the story of a small town who dreamed less than they should, and the few people who did and were the worse for it. It’s the story of unrealized dreams, an unrealized love, and an undone marriage. It’s the story of those who stayed, looking for anything stable in a place where, as the name suggests, everything falls.<br /><br />“‘Power and Control’ Mrs. Whiting repeated, a smile playing at her lips.”<br /><br />The mantra of the most powerful entity in Empire Falls proved to be her undoing. For like the great C.B.’s attempt on the Knox River, one cannot control life’s stream nor tamper with its power. Sometimes we think we do, sometimes we think we can overpower our dreams into fruition by sheer will, sometimes we think we can control life, but that is not always the case. Those times are but momentary lulls in a river raging with life. For if the untamable river of life makes empires, like Mrs. Whiting, fall. The same can happen to you and me. Life is like the River Knox, running, ebbing, and each flow different from the other. <br /><br />“And I’ll tell you something else. What people around here like best about me? They like it that they’re more like me than they are like you. They look at me and they see the town they grew up in. They see their first girlfriend. They see the first high school football game they ever went to. You know what they see when they look at you? That they ain’t good enough. They look at you and see everything they ever done wrong in their lives. They hear you talk and maybe they’re thinking the same thing you are, except they can’t say it like you do and they know they won’t ever get credit. They see you and your buddy the principal with your heads together, deciding how things are gonna be, talking the way you talk and making your little jokes, and they know they’ll never get no place with either of you, not ever. But me? Maybe they might just get someplace with me, and that’s why they like me… They like my attitude, I guess you could say. And you know what? An attitude like yours? An attitude like yours leads to things.” <br /><br />Of all the passages in this remarkable novel, this one had the most powerful impact on me. I confess that I’m guilty of this particular folly. Having gone to one of the best universities in my country, I usually talk fancy or intellectually even when I should not. I see people crease their brows, misunderstand me and feel inferior. When I do, more often than not, I take pride in that. I now abhor this foolishness and the pride along with it. With knowledge comes wisdom, the understanding of its application. For knowledge alone negates itself with the stupidity of pride, then boastfulness. Now I understand that I have no right to look down on people just because my dreams bore fruition. What if it were not the case? How would I feel then? The truly enlightened know never to be boastful, lest the great waves of the river descend upon you and drift your dreams away. One should not turn into a nightmare a good dream. <br /><br />“And there comes a time in your life when you realize that if you don't take the opportunity to be happy, you may never get another chance again.” <br /><br />The thing is unfulfilled dreams, as with Miles, will always stay with you. It will always cast a shadow on your life. But you know what? Dream again. Yes, having your dream unrealized hurts. It feels like shit. But not dreaming again is even worse; it will deflate your will to carry on. Giving up, that’s even shittier. Don’t let the pain of an experience defeat you, let it teach you instead. So dream of a new grill for your diner, dream of losing weight, dream of sending your kids to college, dream of buying all those books you’ve always wanted. Dream lots of things, because some of them are bound to come true. And it gets better. With your experience, you know that not every dream comes to pass. You’re less vulnerable, more mature, and with age, your dreams tend to be less selfish. Because when you were younger, your dreams were more synonymous with wants, now they’re much closer to hope. Maybe fortune is now more inclined to smile upon you. So how do you wake up every morning with a smile? You do so because just then, you were dreaming.<br /><br />“After all, what was the whole wide world but a place for people to yearn for their hearts’ impossible desires, for those desires to become entrenched in defiance of logic, plausibility, and even the passage of time, as eternal as polished marble?”<br /><br />How do you tell yourself “I have a happy life”?<br /><br />Here’s a little secret. You don’t. There never is a happy life. Happiness is always momentary. Sometimes you’ll be happy, sometimes not. What people do wrong is they search for this happiness in their life’s entirety; but the answer is not to look, but to feel. Sight will always show you what you don’t have, what you didn’t become, what you failed to achieve, what’s better than you. But when you close your eyes and dwell about everything, every experience, every moment, you might just feel that little thing called contentment. And when you do, my friend, you’re one of the luckiest people on earth.<br /><br />How do you keep afloat?<br /><br />You stop fighting the current, let the river that is life carry you. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3269479855549661839">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1396026055">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1396026055" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/9822645-jon" class="left imgcol" title="Jon"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1439348441p2/9822645.jpg" alt="Jon" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1396026055?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 04, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/9822645-jon" class="user" title="Jon">Jon</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1396026055">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13387850356398921510">A sprawling, leisurely-told story with a large, well drawn cast of characters, Empire Falls caught and held my interest from the first page. Russo spends a great deal of time setting things up and introducing all the characters and subplots and I found myself truly caring about the characters and what happens to them as I read the book. The book takes place in the fictional Maine town of Empire Falls. Once a thriving manufacturing center, the town has fallen onto hard times due to globalization</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13387850356398921510">A sprawling, leisurely-told story with a large, well drawn cast of characters, Empire Falls caught and held my interest from the first page. Russo spends a great deal of time setting things up and introducing all the characters and subplots and I found myself truly caring about the characters and what happens to them as I read the book. The book takes place in the fictional Maine town of Empire Falls. Once a thriving manufacturing center, the town has fallen onto hard times due to globalization and the outsourcing of the factory jobs that once supported the community. Russo does a good job of painting a picture of a blue-collar community living lives of quiet desperation without resorting to didacticism. There’s definitely poignancy and empathy towards those living on the margins:<br /><br />''They stayed, many of them, because staying was easier and less scary than leaving, and because for a while at least they'd be able to draw unemployment benefits. Others remained out of pride. When the realization dawned that they were the victims of corporate greed and global economic forces, they said, O.K., sure, fine, they'd been fools but they would not, by God, be run out of the town their grandparents and parents had grown up in and called home. The fortunate ones were older, their modest houses nearly paid off; close to retirement, they would limp to the finish line, then help their less fortunate sons and daughters as best they could.''<br /><br />The main character is Miles Roby, a decent man who once had a bright future and a way to escape from his small town, but who ended up back in Empire Falls, managing a failing café and impassively watching as his 20-year marriage fails also. Roby is a man living a life of quiet desperation and who isn’t able to recognize it: “I mean, if I were so unhappy, wouldn’t I know?” Unfortunately, for most of the novel, Miles doesn’t know and it takes some long buried secrets coming to light to rouse him enough to make some needed changes in his life.<br /><br />Don’t get the impression that the book is one long depressing slog. The book is actually very funny with lively dialogue and vibrant secondary characters that are well written. Russo displays a humane sympathy for his characters even as he pokes fun at them and the book paints a poignant picture of small town America <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13387850356398921510">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_30861910">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/30861910" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/54752-john" class="left imgcol" title="John"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1184819140p2/54752.jpg" alt="John" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/30861910?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 26, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/54752-john" class="user" title="John">John</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer30861910">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7457087663122766741">If future archaeologists have nothing but novels written over the past 20 years or so to judge our society by, they will come to the following conclusions:<br />1. Nearly everyone used foul language (although perhaps the future archaeologists won't know that it <em>was</em> foul language).<br />2. Nearly everyone thought about sex most of the time.<br />3. Almost all adults were either divorced, in the process of getting a divorce, or remarried after having been divorced.<br />4. Young people spent a portion of their lives in a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7457087663122766741">If future archaeologists have nothing but novels written over the past 20 years or so to judge our society by, they will come to the following conclusions:<br />1. Nearly everyone used foul language (although perhaps the future archaeologists won't know that it <em>was</em> foul language).<br />2. Nearly everyone thought about sex most of the time.<br />3. Almost all adults were either divorced, in the process of getting a divorce, or remarried after having been divorced.<br />4. Young people spent a portion of their lives in a horrible place called "high school," where all but a tiny elite were ostracized and mocked. (Was every author an outcast in high school?)<br />All of the above applies to Richard Russo's "Empire Falls." It also covers most of what I didn't like about the book.<br />This left a lot to like. Russo offers a compelling narrative that is -- cliche alert -- hard to put down. He presents interesting characters, although perhaps they lack in depth. It's easy to tell who you're supposed to like and who you're not supposed to like.<br />You're supposed to like the central character, Miles Roby, who runs the Empire Grill in Empire Falls, a town in Maine that appears to be in an irreversible decline.<br />One of the central story lines involves Miles and his teenage daughter Christina, aka Tick. This relationship was the most attractive part of the book for me. I wanted to be a dad for a very long time, and I always pictured myself as the father of a daughter. So I tend to vicariously experience this relationship through nauseatingly sentimental movies such as "Father of the Bride" and "My Girl" and even in recent episodes of the "Funky Winkerbean" comic strip.<br />Miles is sort of like the dad I picture myself to be, and Tick is sort of like the daughter I imagine. I like how Miles and Tick find "unintentional humor in the form of gaffes in the Empire Gazette, misspellings in advertisements for local stores, lapses in logic on printed signs like the one on the brick wall that surrounded the old empty shirt factory: NO TRESPASSING WITHOUT PERMISSION." <br />Russo writes some fine sentences, none better than the one at the beginning of Chapter 5, when we learn almost everything there is to know about Miles' father, Max:<br />"The donut shop in Empire Falls had always been one of Max Roby's favorite places because of its smoking policy, which was, 'Go ahead. See if we care.'"<br />Although "Empire Falls" is an easy read, Russo writes a few sentences that leave me saying: How's that again? For example:<br />"What Tick's coming to realize is that in some ways Zack's never been interested in her either, though not, she suspects, in the same way he's not interested in Candace."<br />But maybe that's not such a bad sentence. Perhaps it reflects the way a teenage girl thinks. I wouldn't know.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7457087663122766741">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a href="/user/show/43734196-drew-james" style="font-weight:bold">Drew James</a>

      
<span id="freeTextContainer16715991696540887165">All of the things on that list seemed pretty accurate to me honestly, especially for a small town. I don't think it's about being a high school outcas</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16715991696540887165">All of the things on that list seemed pretty accurate to me honestly, especially for a small town. I don't think it's about being a high school outcast—I think Russo, using Tick's perspective, over-dramatized the social rules and entanglements of high school, to emphasize to us just how misguided the whole thing really is. That's especially evident at the end, when Candace wants to be Tick's friend. At the beginning, she suspected that Candace was afraid of being friends with her since she was too worried about popularity. I do think high school life is very hierarchical, though, and the kids can be very paranoid.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16715991696540887165">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <a href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes" class="user" title="Will Byrnes">Will Byrnes</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer36473841">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6584015316591510310">This is a great American novel, following the intertwined lives of the residents of Empire Falls, Maine. Empire Falls is a declining mill town, lorded over by the baronial Whiting clan. It covers several generations, focusing mostly on the present day and recalling the past. Like all small towns, this is one with secrets, good guys and bad, but all the characters are drawn richly, with respect. There is wisdom here, perception and blindness, short joys and long regrets. This is a book that sings</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6584015316591510310">This is a great American novel, following the intertwined lives of the residents of Empire Falls, Maine. Empire Falls is a declining mill town, lorded over by the baronial Whiting clan. It covers several generations, focusing mostly on the present day and recalling the past. Like all small towns, this is one with secrets, good guys and bad, but all the characters are drawn richly, with respect. There is wisdom here, perception and blindness, short joys and long regrets. This is a book that sings to me, resonating with issues that are personal and real in my life. It is a masterpiece.<br /><br />Some of the characters make moral decisions and take the consequences of their actions. <br /><br />Decisions are made for moral reasons and characters pay willingly for their sins. Working class and upper class interact here in a tale filled with religion, human experience<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6584015316591510310">...more</a>

  <a href="/user/show/388995-caitlin" class="left imgcol" title="Caitlin"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" alt="Caitlin" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/16366495?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 25, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/388995-caitlin" class="user" title="Caitlin">Caitlin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer16366495">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7801044014059762238">The book begins with a brilliant and unforgettable image but becomes increasingly less memorable as the book continues. Russo's style is pleasantly lulling and subtle--appropriate especially here for the description of the slow demise of a midland maine town. But the book goes on too long, and the ending is completely unsatisfying.</span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_157141763">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/157141763" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5155662-ian-pardo" class="left imgcol" title="Ian Pardo"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1301477747p2/5155662.jpg" alt="Ian Pardo" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/157141763?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 29, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5155662-ian-pardo" class="user" title="Ian Pardo">Ian Pardo</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer157141763">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11078694346678192699">As I put down EMPIRE FALLS after its last page, it suddenly dawned on me: "I have to read another book now, don't I." From the first page, I fell in love with the wonderful but flawed characters of Richard Russo's Pulitzer Prize-winner, and leaving the town of Empire Falls, Maine hit me with the force of parting with a dear friend. <br /></span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_23858732">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23858732" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1212946-jane" class="left imgcol" title="Jane"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" alt="Jane" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <a href="/user/show/1212946-jane" class="user" title="Jane">Jane</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer23858732">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5389504248502489827">A small town in Maine. I could almost describe it from memory of my early years as a seasonal visitor there. The characters are so real, and we have a "silver fox" in our community, too. Teachers have to love the description of the art teacher's scene. The novel is wry and poignant, one of the best.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_25703741">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/25703741" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/903066-johnny" class="left imgcol" title="Johnny"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442457109p2/903066.jpg" alt="Johnny" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/25703741?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 27, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/903066-johnny" class="user" title="Johnny">Johnny</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/903066-johnny?shelf=light-novels" class="actionLinkLite">light-novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer25703741">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16154215347155525743">"Diverting one's attention from the past was not the same as envisioning and embarking upon a future." (p. 19) While this seems to be the theme of <em>Empire Falls</em> by Richard Russo, my biggest problem with this novel is that there doesn't seem to be any move toward the future in this novel. Everyone in the book seems to be so entangled in the misdeeds of their parents and grandparents, as well as their own, that they can't cut free and live that future.<br /><br />Even when the hopes and dreams of the parents a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16154215347155525743">"Diverting one's attention from the past was not the same as envisioning and embarking upon a future." (p. 19) While this seems to be the theme of <em>Empire Falls</em> by Richard Russo, my biggest problem with this novel is that there doesn't seem to be any move toward the future in this novel. Everyone in the book seems to be so entangled in the misdeeds of their parents and grandparents, as well as their own, that they can't cut free and live that future.<br /><br />Even when the hopes and dreams of the parents are that the characters get loose for that future, the offspring seem doomed to betray that hope. Even when the offspring do take a few steps forward, the snare of their background pulls them back into a cesspool of despair that would make Camus or Kierkegaard seem like optimists.<br /><br />At the conclusion of this book, I was as depressed as I was at any time since my first viewing of <em>The Last Picture Show</em>. As a minister, I found this novel to be the best presentation of the biblical idea that "the wage of sin is death" since that classic Bogdonavich film.<br /><br />To be sure, there were touching moments that were artistically expressed enough to bring tears to my eyes and there were occasional flashes of insights that made it worth reading. Two such insights will be in upcoming sermons. Here are the ones I most enjoyed:<br /><br />"'When I was a little boy,' Father Mark said, 'I used to think God actually lived up there.'<br /><br />'In the steeple?' Miles said.<br /><br />Father Mark nodded. 'I thought when we sang hymns we were calling to Him to come down and be among us. Which, of course we were. But the literal proximity was reassuring.'"<br /><br />But it goes on.<br /><br />"'God Himself, a couple stories up...so close.'<br /><br />'I was just thinking how far away it is,' Miles admitted."<br /><br />It goes on to suggest that Miles was more worried about falling, but then he goes on to think about the conversation.<br /><br />"Interesting, Miles thought. Like himself, Father Mark, as a child, had been reassured by the imagined proximity of God, whereas adults, perhaps because they so often were up to no good, took more comfort from His remoteness." (pp. 44-45)<br /><br />Later, on pages 198-199, the same priest admits that he once thought of God as being like his maternal grandmother. It seems the old lady gave gifts of cash to her grandchildren for Christmas. She claimed to be able to see into their hearts and to give them money based on how much they loved her. Of course, the thing is that she merely placed the cash for the grandchildren based on her relationship to their parents. It wasn't an opportunity to give based on love, but a vindictive opportunity to get at her children through her grandchildren.<br /><br />The implication is that God is precisely the opposite of the vindictive grandmother. He really does give because He loves us and, even though He can see into the heart, He gives on the basis of His love, not ours.<br /><br />Of course, there is also some bad theology in the book. On page 261, a prayer is said to be answered on the basis "God will sometimes respond to a request that's carelessly phrased." I think Russo has confused "magic" and "prayer." The devil looks for a carelessly phrased request in order to torture the petitioner, but God responds according to His wisdom, not ours.<br /><br />As you can see, <em>Empire Falls</em> was stimulating to me, but not satisfying, colorful but not constructed well enough to be considered a solid work.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16154215347155525743">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
